<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Cattle</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>

<p><b>Cattle</b>—colloquially <b>cows</b>—are the most common type of large <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domesticated</a> <a href="/wiki/Ungulate" title="Ungulate">ungulates</a>. They are a prominent modern member of the <a href="/wiki/Subfamily" title="Subfamily">subfamily</a> <a href="/wiki/Bovinae" title="Bovinae">Bovinae</a>, are the most widespread <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Bos" title="Bos">Bos</a></i>, and are most commonly classified collectively as <i><b>Bos taurus</b></i>.
</p><p>Cattle are commonly raised as <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a>; for meat (<a href="/wiki/Beef" title="Beef">beef</a> or <a href="/wiki/Veal" title="Veal">veal</a>, see <a href="/wiki/Beef_cattle" title="Beef cattle">beef cattle</a>), for <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Dairy_cattle" title="Dairy cattle">dairy cattle</a>), and for <a href="/wiki/Hide_(skin)" title="Hide (skin)">hides</a>, which are used to make <a href="/wiki/Leather" title="Leather">leather</a>. They are used as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riding_animal" title="Riding animal">riding animals</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Draft_animal" title="Draft animal">draft animals</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ox" title="Ox">oxen</a> or <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bullock" title="wikt:bullock">bullocks</a>, which pull <a href="/wiki/Cart" title="Cart">carts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plow" title="Plow">plows</a> and other implements). Another product of cattle is <a href="/wiki/Cow_dung" title="Cow dung">dung</a>, which can be used to create <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">manure</a> or <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a>. In some regions, such as parts of India, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cattle_in_religion" title="Cattle in religion">cattle have significant religious meaning</a>. Cattle, mostly small breeds such as the <a href="/wiki/Miniature_Zebu" title="Miniature Zebu">Miniature Zebu</a>, are kept as <a href="/wiki/Pet" title="Pet">pets</a>.
</p><p>Around 10,500 years ago, cattle were domesticated from as few as 80 progenitors in southeast Turkey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HowNowCow_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-HowNowCow-1">[1]</a></sup> According to an estimate from 2011, there are 1.4 billion cattle in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> In 2009, cattle became one of the first livestock animals to have a fully mapped <a href="/wiki/Bovine_genome" title="Bovine genome">genome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cattle_genome_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cattle_genome-3">[3]</a></sup>  Some consider cattle the oldest form of wealth, and <a href="/wiki/Cattle_raiding" title="Cattle raiding">cattle raiding</a> consequently one of the earliest forms of theft.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a href="#Oxen"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Oxen</span></a></li>

</li>

</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Bos" title="Bos">Bos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bovinae" title="Bovinae">Bovinae</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cow_female_black_white.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1680" data-file-width="2190" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Cow_female_black_white.jpg/220px-Cow_female_black_white.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Cow_female_black_white.jpg/330px-Cow_female_black_white.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Cow_female_black_white.jpg/440px-Cow_female_black_white.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cow_female_black_white.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Holstein_Friesian_cattle" title="Holstein Friesian cattle">Holstein Friesian</a> cow, a typical member of the <i>Bos taurus taurus</i> sub-species</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zubron2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="293" data-file-width="324" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Zubron2.jpg/220px-Zubron2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b6/Zubron2.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zubron2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/%C5%BBubro%C5%84" title="Żubroń">Żubroń</a>, a <a href="/wiki/European_bison" title="European bison">wisent</a> and cattle hybrid</div></div></div>
<p>Cattle were originally identified as three separate species: <i>Bos taurus</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Taurine_cattle" title="Taurine cattle">European or "taurine" cattle</a> (including similar types from Africa and Asia); <i>Bos indicus</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Zebu" title="Zebu">zebu</a>; and the extinct <i>Bos primigenius</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Aurochs" title="Aurochs">aurochs</a>. The aurochs is ancestral to both zebu and taurine cattle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> These have been reclassified as one species, <i>Bos taurus</i>, with three subspecies: <i>Bos taurus primigenius</i>, <i>Bos taurus indicus</i>, and <i>Bos taurus taurus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSW3_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSW3-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Complicating the matter is the ability of cattle to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interbreed" title="Interbreed">interbreed</a> with other closely related species. Hybrid individuals and even breeds exist, not only between taurine cattle and zebu (such as the <a href="/wiki/Sanga_cattle" title="Sanga cattle">sanga cattle</a>, <i>Bos taurus africanus</i>), but also between one or both of these and some other members of the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Bos" title="Bos">Bos</a></i> – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yak" title="Yak">yaks</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Dzo" title="Dzo">dzo</a> or yattle<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Banteng" title="Banteng">banteng</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gaur" title="Gaur">gaur</a>. Hybrids such as the <a href="/wiki/Beefalo" title="Beefalo">beefalo</a> breed can even occur between taurine cattle and either species of <a href="/wiki/Bison" title="Bison">bison</a>, leading some authors to consider them part of the genus <i>Bos</i>, as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The hybrid origin of some types may not be obvious – for example, <a href="/wiki/Genetic_testing" title="Genetic testing">genetic testing</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_Lulu" title="Dwarf Lulu">Dwarf Lulu</a> breed, the only taurine-type cattle in Nepal, found them to be a mix of taurine cattle, zebu, and yak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> However, cattle cannot be successfully hybridized with more distantly related bovines such as <a href="/wiki/Water_buffalo" title="Water buffalo">water buffalo</a> or <a href="/wiki/African_buffalo" title="African buffalo">African buffalo</a>.
</p><p>The aurochs originally ranged throughout Europe, North Africa, and much of Asia. In historical times, its range became restricted to Europe, and the last known individual died in <a href="/wiki/Mazovia" title="Mazovia">Mazovia</a>, Poland, in about 1627.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Breeders have attempted to recreate cattle of similar appearance to aurochs by crossing traditional types of domesticated cattle, creating the <a href="/wiki/Heck_cattle" title="Heck cattle">Heck cattle</a> breed.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The noun <i>cattle</i> (which is treated as a plural and has no singular) encompasses both sexes. The singular, <i>cow</i>, technically means the female, the male being <i>bull</i>. The plural form <i>cows</i> is sometimes used colloquially to refer to both sexes collectively, as <i>e.g.</i> in a herd, but that usage can be misleading as the speaker's intent may indeed be just the females. The bovine species <i>per se</i> is clearly dimorphic.
</p><p><i>Cattle</i> did not originate as the term for bovine animals. It was borrowed from <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Norman_language" title="Anglo-Norman language">Anglo-Norman</a> <i lang="xno" title="Anglo-Norman language text">catel</i>, itself from medieval Latin <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">capitale</i> 'principal sum of money, capital', itself derived in turn from Latin <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">caput</i> 'head'. <i>Cattle</i> originally meant movable <a href="/wiki/Personal_property" title="Personal property">personal property</a>, especially livestock of any kind, as opposed to <a href="/wiki/Real_property" title="Real property">real property</a> (the land, which also included wild or small free-roaming animals such as chickens — they were sold as part of the land).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The word is a variant of <i><a href="/wiki/Personal_property" title="Personal property">chattel</a></i> (a unit of personal property) and closely related to <i><a href="/wiki/Capital_(economics)" title="Capital (economics)">capital</a></i> in the economic sense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The term replaced earlier <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <i lang="ang" title="Old English language text">feoh</i> 'cattle, property', which survives today as <i>fee</i> (cf. German: <i lang="de">Vieh</i>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>: <i lang="nl">vee</i>, <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Gothic</a>: <span lang="got">faihu</span>).
</p><p>The word "cow" came via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_language" title="Anglo-Saxon language">Anglo-Saxon</a> <i lang="ang" title="Old English language text">cū</i> (plural <i>cȳ</i>), from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_Indo-European" title="Common Indo-European">Common Indo-European</a> <span class="Unicode" lang="ine" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><i>gʷōus</i></span> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genitive" title="Genitive">genitive</a> <span class="Unicode" lang="ine" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><i>gʷowés</i></span>) = "a bovine animal", compare <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <i lang="fa-Latn">gâv</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a>: <i lang="sa-Latn">go-</i>, <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>: <i lang="cy">buwch</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The plural <i>cȳ</i> became <i>ki</i> or <i>kie</i> in Middle English, and an additional plural ending was often added, giving <i>kine</i>, <i>kien</i>, but also <i>kies</i>, <i>kuin</i> and others. This is the origin of the now archaic English plural, "kine". The <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots language</a> singular is coo or <a class="extiw" href="https://sco.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cou" title="sco:Cou">cou</a>, and the plural is "kye".
</p><p>In older English sources such as the <a href="/wiki/King_James_Version" title="King James Version">King James Version</a> of the Bible, "cattle" refers to livestock, as opposed to "deer" which refers to wildlife.  "Wild cattle" may refer to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feral_organism" title="Feral organism">feral</a> cattle or to undomesticated species of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Bos" title="Bos">Bos</a></i>.  Today, when used without any other qualifier, the modern meaning of "cattle" is usually restricted to domesticated bovines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span></h2>
<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg/40px-Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg/60px-Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg/80px-Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist">Look up <i><b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cattle" title="wiktionary:cattle">cattle</a></b></i> or <i><b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cow" title="wiktionary:cow">cow</a></b></i> in Wiktionary, the free dictionary.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Ongole_Bull_of_Moses.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="567" data-file-width="793" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/The_Ongole_Bull_of_Moses.jpg/220px-The_Ongole_Bull_of_Moses.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/The_Ongole_Bull_of_Moses.jpg/330px-The_Ongole_Bull_of_Moses.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/The_Ongole_Bull_of_Moses.jpg/440px-The_Ongole_Bull_of_Moses.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Ongole_Bull_of_Moses.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Ongole_cattle" title="Ongole cattle">Ongole</a> bull</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hereford_bull_large.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="369" data-file-width="550" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Hereford_bull_large.jpg/220px-Hereford_bull_large.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Hereford_bull_large.jpg/330px-Hereford_bull_large.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Hereford_bull_large.jpg/440px-Hereford_bull_large.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hereford_bull_large.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Hereford_cattle" title="Hereford cattle">Hereford</a> bull</div></div></div>
<p>In general, the same words are used in different parts of the world, but with minor differences in the definitions.  The terminology described here contrasts the differences in definition between the United Kingdom and other British-influenced parts of the world such as Canada, Australia, New Zealand, Ireland and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cterm_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-cterm-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><span id="bull"></span>An "intact" (i.e., not <a href="/wiki/Castration" title="Castration">castrated</a>) adult male is called a <i>bull</i>.  A wild, young, unmarked bull is known as a <i>micky</i> in Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coupe_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coupe-16">[16]</a></sup> An un<a href="/wiki/Livestock_branding" title="Livestock branding">branded</a> bovine of either sex is called a <i>maverick</i> in the US and Canada.</li>
<li>An adult female that has had a calf (or two, depending on regional usage) is a <i>cow</i>.</li>
<li><span id="heifer"></span>A young female before she has had a calf of her own<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> and is under three years of age is called a <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/heifer" title="wikt:heifer">heifer</a></i> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span><span title="/ər/: 'er' in 'letter'">ər</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">HEF</span>-ər</i></a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MD_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-MD-18">[18]</a></sup>  A young female that has had only one calf is occasionally called a <i>first-calf heifer</i>.</li>
<li>Young cattle of both sexes are called <i><a href="/wiki/Calf" title="Calf">calves</a></i> until they are <a href="/wiki/Weaning" title="Weaning">weaned</a>, then <i>weaners</i> until they are a year old in some areas; in other areas, particularly with male beef cattle, they may be known as <i>feeder calves</i> or simply feeders.  After that, they are referred to as <i>yearlings</i> or <i>stirks</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> if between one and two years of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></li>
<li>A castrated male is called a <i>steer</i> in the United States; older steers are often called <i>bullocks</i> in other parts of the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> but in North America this term refers to a young bull.  Piker bullocks are micky bulls (uncastrated young male bulls) that were caught, castrated and then later lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coupe_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Coupe-16">[16]</a></sup>  In Australia, the term <i>Japanese ox</i> is used for grain-fed steers in the weight range of 500 to 650 kg that are destined for the Japanese meat trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> In North America, draft cattle under four years old are called working steers.  Improper or late castration on a bull results in it becoming a coarse steer known as a <i>stag</i> in Australia, Canada and New Zealand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> In some countries, an incompletely castrated male is known also as a <i><a href="/wiki/Ridgling" title="Ridgling">rig</a></i>.</li>
<li>A castrated male (occasionally a female or in some areas a bull) kept for draft or riding purposes is called an <i><a href="/wiki/Ox" title="Ox">ox</a></i> (plural oxen); <i>ox</i> may also be used to refer to some carcass products from any adult cattle, such as ox-hide, ox-blood, oxtail, or ox-liver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MD_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-MD-18">[18]</a></sup></li>
<li>A <i>springer</i> is a cow or heifer close to calving.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></li>
<li>In all cattle species, a female twin of a bull usually becomes an infertile partial <a href="/wiki/Intersex" title="Intersex">intersex</a>, and is called a <i><a href="/wiki/Freemartin" title="Freemartin">freemartin</a></i>.</li>
<li>Neat (horned oxen, from which <a href="/wiki/Neatsfoot_oil" title="Neatsfoot oil">neatsfoot oil</a> is derived), beef (young ox) and beefing (young animal fit for slaughtering) are obsolete terms, although <i>poll</i>, <i>pollard</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Polled_livestock" title="Polled livestock">polled cattle</a></i> are still terms in use for naturally hornless animals, or in some areas also for those that have been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disbudding" title="Disbudding">disbudded</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dehorning" title="Dehorning">dehorned</a>.</li>
<li>Cattle raised for human consumption are called <i><a href="/wiki/Beef_cattle" title="Beef cattle">beef cattle</a></i>. Within the American beef cattle industry, the older term beef (plural beeves) is still used to refer to an animal of either sex. Some Australian, Canadian, New Zealand and British people use the term <i>beast</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></li>
<li>Cattle bred specifically for milk production are called <i>milking</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Dairy_cattle" title="Dairy cattle">dairy cattle</a></i>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cterm_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-cterm-15">[15]</a></sup> a cow kept to provide milk for one family may be called a <i><a href="/wiki/House_cow" title="House cow">house cow</a></i> or <i>milker</i>. A <i>fresh cow</i> is a dairy term for a cow or first-calf heifer who has recently given birth, or "freshened."</li>
<li>The adjective applying to cattle in general is usually <i>bovine</i>. The terms <i>bull</i>, <i>cow</i> and <i>calf</i> are also used by extension to denote the sex or age of other large animals, including whales, <a href="/wiki/Hippopotamus" title="Hippopotamus">hippopotamuses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">camels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elk" title="Elk">elk</a> and elephants.</li></ul>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_animal_names" title="List of animal names">List of animal names</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Singular_terminology_issue">Singular terminology issue</span></h3>
<p>"Cattle" can only be used in the <a href="/wiki/Plural" title="Plural">plural</a> and not in the <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">singular</a>: it is a <a href="/wiki/Plurale_tantum" title="Plurale tantum">plurale tantum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED1_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED1-26">[26]</a></sup> Thus one may refer to "three cattle" or "some cattle", but not "one cattle". No universally used singular form in modern English of <i>cattle</i> exists, other than the sex- and age-specific terms such as cow, bull, steer and heifer.  Historically, "ox" was not a sex-specific term for adult cattle, but generally this is now used only for <a href="/wiki/Ox" title="Ox">working cattle</a>, especially adult castrated males. The term is also incorporated into the names of other species, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musk_ox" title="Musk ox">musk ox</a> and "grunting ox" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yak" title="Yak">yak</a>), and is used in some areas to describe certain cattle products such as ox-hide and <a href="/wiki/Oxtail" title="Oxtail">oxtail</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED2_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED2-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brahman_Baby.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="790" data-file-width="1050" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Brahman_Baby.jpg/220px-Brahman_Baby.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Brahman_Baby.jpg/330px-Brahman_Baby.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Brahman_Baby.jpg/440px-Brahman_Baby.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brahman_Baby.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahman_(cattle)" title="Brahman (cattle)">Brahman</a> calf</div></div></div>
<p><i>Cow</i> is in general use as a singular for the collective <i>cattle.</i> The word <i>cow</i> is easy to use when a singular is needed and the sex is unknown or irrelevant – when "there is a cow in the road", for example. Further, any herd of fully mature cattle in or near a <a href="/wiki/Pasture" title="Pasture">pasture</a> is statistically likely to consist mostly of cows, so the term is probably accurate even in the restrictive sense.  Other than the few bulls needed for breeding, the vast majority of male cattle are castrated as calves and are used as <a href="/wiki/Ox" title="Ox">oxen</a> or slaughtered for meat before the age of three years.  Thus, in a pastured herd, any calves or herd bulls usually are clearly distinguishable from the cows due to distinctively different sizes and clear anatomical differences. Merriam-Webster and Oxford Living Dictionaries recognize the sex-nonspecific use of <i>cow</i> as an alternate definition,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> whereas Collins and the OED do not.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colloquial" title="Colloquial">Colloquially</a>, more general non<a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">specific</a> terms may denote cattle when a singular form is needed. Australian, New Zealand and British farmers use the term <i>beast</i> or <i>cattle beast</i>. <i>Bovine</i> is also used in Britain. The term <i>critter</i> is common in the western United States and Canada, particularly when referring to young cattle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> In some areas of the American South (particularly the Appalachian region), where both dairy and beef cattle are present, an individual animal was once called a "beef critter", though that term is becoming <a href="/wiki/Archaism" title="Archaism">archaic</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_terminology">Other terminology</span></h3>
<table class="mbox-small" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></div></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Mudchute_cow_1.ogg" title="File:Mudchute cow 1.ogg">A cow's moo</a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="2.1942857142857" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Mudchute_cow_1.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:220px"><source data-bandwidth="94701" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (95 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/48/Mudchute_cow_1.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="131880" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/48/Mudchute_cow_1.ogg/Mudchute_cow_1.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0"></div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr/><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Cattle raised for human consumption are called <i><a href="/wiki/Beef_cattle" title="Beef cattle">beef cattle</a></i>. Within the beef cattle industry in parts of the United States, the term <i>beef</i> (plural <i>beeves</i>) is still used in its archaic sense to refer to an animal of either sex. Cows of certain breeds that are kept for the milk they give are called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dairy_cow" title="Dairy cow">dairy cows</a></i> or <i>milking cows</i> (formerly <i>milch cows</i>).  Most young male offspring of dairy cows are sold for <a href="/wiki/Veal" title="Veal">veal</a>, and may be referred to as veal calves.
</p><p>The term <i>dogies</i> is used to describe orphaned calves in the context of ranch work in the <a href="/wiki/Western_United_States" title="Western United States">American West</a>, as in "Keep them dogies moving".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> In some places, a cow kept to provide milk for one family is called a "house cow".  Other obsolete terms for cattle include "neat" (this use survives in "<a href="/wiki/Neatsfoot_oil" title="Neatsfoot oil">neatsfoot oil</a>", extracted from the feet and legs of cattle), and "beefing" (young animal fit for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slaughter_(livestock)" title="Slaughter (livestock)">slaughter</a>).
</p><p>An <a href="/wiki/Onomatopoeia" title="Onomatopoeia">onomatopoeic</a> term for one of the most common <a href="/wiki/List_of_animal_sounds" title="List of animal sounds">sounds</a> made by cattle is <i>moo</i> (also called <i>lowing</i>). There are a number of other sounds made by cattle, including calves <i>bawling</i>, and bulls <i>bellowing</i>. Bawling is most common for cows after weaning of a calf. The <a href="/wiki/Bullroarer" title="Bullroarer">bullroarer</a> makes a sound similar to a bull's territorial call.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anatomy">Anatomy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A1%D0%BA%D0%B5%D0%BB%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%BC%D0%B0%D1%88%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B9_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%8B.jpg"><img alt="Bones are mounted on a black board" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3240" data-file-width="4320" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/%D0%A1%D0%BA%D0%B5%D0%BB%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%BC%D0%B0%D1%88%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B9_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%8B.jpg/220px-%D0%A1%D0%BA%D0%B5%D0%BB%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%BC%D0%B0%D1%88%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B9_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%8B.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/%D0%A1%D0%BA%D0%B5%D0%BB%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%BC%D0%B0%D1%88%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B9_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%8B.jpg/330px-%D0%A1%D0%BA%D0%B5%D0%BB%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%BC%D0%B0%D1%88%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B9_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%8B.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/%D0%A1%D0%BA%D0%B5%D0%BB%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%BC%D0%B0%D1%88%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B9_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%8B.jpg/440px-%D0%A1%D0%BA%D0%B5%D0%BB%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%BC%D0%B0%D1%88%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B9_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%8B.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A1%D0%BA%D0%B5%D0%BB%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%BC%D0%B0%D1%88%D0%BD%D0%B5%D0%B9_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%8B.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Displayed skeleton of a domestic cow</div></div></div>
<p>Cattle are large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quadrupedal" title="Quadrupedal">quadrupedal</a> <a href="/wiki/Ungulate" title="Ungulate">ungulate</a> <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cloven_hooves" title="Cloven hooves">cloven hooves</a>. Most breeds have <a href="/wiki/Horn_(anatomy)" title="Horn (anatomy)">horns</a>, which can be as large as the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Longhorn" title="Texas Longhorn">Texas Longhorn</a> or small like a <a href="/wiki/Scur" title="Scur">scur</a>. Careful genetic selection has allowed <a href="/wiki/Polled_livestock" title="Polled livestock">polled</a> (hornless) cattle to become widespread.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Modelo_didatico_bovino_correto.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2245" data-file-width="3421" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Modelo_didatico_bovino_correto.jpg/220px-Modelo_didatico_bovino_correto.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Modelo_didatico_bovino_correto.jpg/330px-Modelo_didatico_bovino_correto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Modelo_didatico_bovino_correto.jpg/440px-Modelo_didatico_bovino_correto.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Modelo_didatico_bovino_correto.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anatomy model of a cow</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Digestive_system">Digestive system</span></h4>
<p>Cattle are <a href="/wiki/Ruminant" title="Ruminant">ruminants</a>, meaning their <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_tract" title="Gastrointestinal tract">digestive system</a> is highly specialized to allow the use of poorly digestible plants as food. Cattle have one <a href="/wiki/Stomach" title="Stomach">stomach</a> with four compartments, the <a href="/wiki/Rumen" title="Rumen">rumen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reticulum_(anatomy)" title="Reticulum (anatomy)">reticulum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omasum" title="Omasum">omasum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Abomasum" title="Abomasum">abomasum</a>, with the rumen being the largest compartment.
The reticulum, the smallest compartment, is known as the "honeycomb". Cattle sometimes consume metal objects which are deposited in the reticulum and irritation from the metal objects causes <a href="/wiki/Hardware_disease" title="Hardware disease">hardware disease</a>. The omasum's main function is to absorb water and nutrients from the digestible feed. The omasum is known as the "many plies". The abomasum is like the human stomach; this is why it is known as the "true stomach".
</p><p>Cattle are known for <a href="/wiki/Regurgitation_(digestion)" title="Regurgitation (digestion)">regurgitating</a> and re-chewing their food, known as <a href="/wiki/Cud" title="Cud">cud</a> chewing, like most ruminants.  While the animal is feeding, the food is swallowed without being chewed and goes into the rumen for storage until the animal can find a quiet place to continue the digestion process.  The food is regurgitated, a mouthful at a time, back up to the mouth, where the food, now called the <a href="/wiki/Cud" title="Cud">cud</a>, is chewed by the molars, grinding down the coarse vegetation to small particles.  The cud is then swallowed again and further digested by specialized <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> in the rumen. These microbes are primarily responsible for decomposing <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volatile_fatty_acids" title="Volatile fatty acids">volatile fatty acids</a> cattle use as their primary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metabolic" title="Metabolic">metabolic</a> fuel. The microbes inside the rumen also synthesize <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> from non-protein <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogenous</a> sources, such as <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>. As these microbes reproduce in the rumen, older generations die and their cells continue on through the digestive tract. These cells are then partially digested in the small intestines, allowing cattle to gain a high-quality protein source. These features allow cattle to thrive on <a href="/wiki/Poaceae" title="Poaceae">grasses</a> and other tough vegetation.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gestation_and_size">Gestation and size</span></h4>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gestation_period" title="Gestation period">gestation period</a> for a cow is about nine months long. A newborn calf's size can vary among breeds, but a typical calf weighs between 25 to 45 kg (55 to 99 lb). Adult size and weight vary significantly among breeds and sex. Steers are generally killed before reaching 750 kg (1,650 lb). Breeding stock may be allowed a longer lifespan, occasionally living as long as 25 years. The oldest recorded cow, <a href="/wiki/Big_Bertha_(cow)" title="Big Bertha (cow)">Big Bertha</a>, died at the age of 48 in 1993.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction">Reproduction</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bovine_reproductive_system.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3391" data-file-width="2268" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Bovine_reproductive_system.jpg/140px-Bovine_reproductive_system.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Bovine_reproductive_system.jpg/210px-Bovine_reproductive_system.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Bovine_reproductive_system.jpg/280px-Bovine_reproductive_system.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bovine_reproductive_system.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reproductive system of a bovine female.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ox_testis-_FMVZ_USP-14.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3557" data-file-width="2381" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Ox_testis-_FMVZ_USP-14.jpg/140px-Ox_testis-_FMVZ_USP-14.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Ox_testis-_FMVZ_USP-14.jpg/210px-Ox_testis-_FMVZ_USP-14.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Ox_testis-_FMVZ_USP-14.jpg/280px-Ox_testis-_FMVZ_USP-14.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ox_testis-_FMVZ_USP-14.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ox testis.</div></div></div>
<p>On farms it is very common to use <a href="/wiki/Artificial_insemination" title="Artificial insemination">artificial insemination</a> (AI),  a <a href="/wiki/Assisted_reproductive_technology" title="Assisted reproductive technology">medically assisted reproduction technique</a> consisting of the artificial deposition of <a href="/wiki/Semen" title="Semen">semen</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Female_Reproductive_System" title="Female Reproductive System">female's genital tract</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hopper2014_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hopper2014-33">[33]</a></sup>  It is used in cases where the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spermatozoa" title="Spermatozoa">spermatozoa</a> can not reach the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fallopian_tubes" title="Fallopian tubes">fallopian tubes</a> or simply by choice of the owner of the animal. It consists of transferring, to the <a href="/wiki/Uterus" title="Uterus">uterine cavity</a>, spermatozoa previously collected and processed, with the selection of morphologically more normal and mobile spermatozoa.
</p><p>A cow's <a href="/wiki/Udder" title="Udder">udder</a> contains two pairs of <a href="/wiki/Mammary_gland" title="Mammary gland">mammary glands</a>, (commonly referred to as <i>teats</i>) creating four "quarters".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The front ones are referred to as <i>fore quarters</i> and the rear ones <i>rear quarters</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Bull#Reproductive_anatomy" title="Bull">Bull § Reproductive anatomy</a></div>
<p>Bulls become fertile at about seven months of age.  Their fertility is closely related to the size of their <a href="/wiki/Testicle" title="Testicle">testicles</a>, and one simple test of fertility is to measure the circumference of the scrotum: a young bull is likely to be fertile once this reaches 28 centimetres (11 in); that of a fully adult bull may be over 40 centimetres (16 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bulls have a fibro-elastic penis. Given the small amount of erectile tissue, there is little enlargement after erection. The penis is quite rigid when non-erect, and becomes even more rigid during erection. Protrusion is not affected much by erection, but more by relaxation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retractor_penis_muscle" title="Retractor penis muscle">retractor penis muscle</a> and straightening of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sigmoid_flexure" title="Sigmoid flexure">sigmoid flexure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sarkar_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sarkar-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Induced_ovulation_(animals)" title="Induced ovulation (animals)">Induced ovulation</a> can be manipulated to produce farming benefits. For example, to synchronise ovulation of the cattle to benefit dairy farming.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Weight">Weight</span></h3>
<p>The world record for the heaviest bull was 1,740 kg (3,840 lb), a <a href="/wiki/Chianina" title="Chianina">Chianina</a> named Donetto, when he was exhibited at the <a href="/wiki/Arezzo" title="Arezzo">Arezzo</a> show in 1955.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-41">[41]</a></sup> The heaviest steer was eight-year-old ‘Old Ben’, a <a href="/wiki/Shorthorn" title="Shorthorn">Shorthorn</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hereford_Cattle" title="Hereford Cattle">Hereford</a> cross weighing in at 2,140 kg (4,720 lb) in 1910.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>The weight of adult cattle varies, depending on the breed. Smaller kinds, such as Dexter and Jersey adults, range between 272 to 454 kg (600 to 1,000 lb). Large Continental breeds, such as Charolais, Marchigiana, Belgian Blue and Chianina, adults range from 635 to 1,134 kg (1,400 to 2,500 lb). British breeds, such as Hereford, Angus, and Shorthorn, mature between 454 to 907 kg (1,000 to 2,000 lb), occasionally higher, particularly with Angus and Hereford.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bulls will be a bit larger than cows of the same breed by a few hundred kilograms. Chianina bulls can weigh up to  1,500 kg (3,300 lb); British bulls, such as Angus and Hereford, can weigh as little as 907 kg (2,000 lb) to as much as 1,361 kg (3,000 lb).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>It is difficult to generalize or average out the weight of all cattle because different kinds have different averages of weights. However, according to some sources, the average weight of all cattle is 753 kg (1,660 lb).  Finishing steers in the feedlot average about 640 kg (1,410 lb); cows about 725 kg (1,600 lb), and bulls about 1,090 kg (2,400 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, the average weight of beef cattle has steadily increased, especially since the 1970s, requiring the building of new slaughterhouses able to handle larger carcasses. New packing plants in the 1980s stimulated a large increase in cattle weights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Before 1790 beef cattle averaged only 160 kg (350 lb) net; and thereafter weights climbed steadily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cognition">Cognition</span></h2>
<p>In laboratory studies, young cattle are able to memorize the locations of several food sources and retain this memory for at least 8 hours, although this declined after 12 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey1989_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey1989-48">[48]</a></sup>  Fifteen-month-old heifers learn more quickly than adult cows which have had either one or two calvings, but their longer-term memory is less stable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>  Mature cattle perform well in spatial learning tasks and have a good long-term memory in these tests.  Cattle tested in a <a href="/wiki/Radial_arm_maze" title="Radial arm maze">radial arm maze</a> are able to remember the locations of high-quality food for at least 30 days.  Although they initially learn to avoid low-quality food, this memory diminishes over the same duration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mendl2009first_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mendl2009first-50">[50]</a></sup>  Under less artificial testing conditions, young cattle showed they were able to remember the location of feed for at least 48 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>  Cattle can make an association between a visual stimulus and food within 1 day – memory of this association can be retained for 1 year, despite a slight decay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Calves are capable of discrimination learning<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> and adult cattle compare favourably with small mammals in their learning ability in the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Closed-field_Test&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Closed-field Test (page does not exist)">Closed-field Test</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kilgour1981_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kilgour1981-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>They are also able to discriminate between familiar individuals, and among humans.  Cattle can tell the difference between familiar and unfamiliar animals of the same species (conspecifics).  Studies show they behave less aggressively toward familiar individuals when they are forming a new group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coulon2011_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coulon2011-55">[55]</a></sup>  Calves can also discriminate between humans based on previous experience, as shown by approaching those who handled them positively and avoiding those who handled them aversively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Passille1996_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Passille1996-56">[56]</a></sup> Although cattle can discriminate between humans by their faces alone, they also use other cues such as the color of clothes when these are available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mendl2009second_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mendl2009second-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>In audio play-back studies, calves prefer their own mother's vocalizations compared to the vocalizations of an unfamiliar mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>In laboratory studies using images, cattle can discriminate between images of the heads of cattle and other animal species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coulon2007_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coulon2007-59">[59]</a></sup> They are also able to distinguish between familiar and unfamiliar conspecifics.  Furthermore, they are able to <a href="/wiki/Categorization" title="Categorization">categorize</a> images as familiar and unfamiliar individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coulon2011_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Coulon2011-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>When mixed with other individuals, <a href="/wiki/Cloning" title="Cloning">cloned</a> calves from the same donor form subgroups, indicating that kin discrimination occurs and may be a basis of grouping behaviour.  It has also been shown using images of cattle that both artificially inseminated and cloned calves have similar cognitive capacities of kin and non-kin discrimination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coulon2010x_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coulon2010x-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cattle can recognize familiar individuals. Visual individual recognition is a more complex mental process than visual discrimination.  It requires the recollection of the learned idiosyncratic identity of an individual that has been previously encountered and the formation of a mental representation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hagen2003_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hagen2003-61">[61]</a></sup> By using 2-dimensional images of the heads of one cow (face, profiles, ¾ views), all the tested heifers showed individual recognition of familiar and unfamiliar individuals from their own breed.   Furthermore, almost all the heifers recognized unknown individuals from different breeds, although this was achieved with greater difficulty.  Individual recognition was most difficult when the visual features of the breed being tested were quite different from the breed in the image, for example, the breed being tested had no spots whereas the image was of a spotted breed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coulon2009_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coulon2009-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cattle use <a href="/wiki/Lateralization_of_brain_function" title="Lateralization of brain function">visual/brain lateralisation</a> in their visual scanning of novel and familiar stimuli.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>  Domestic cattle prefer to view novel stimuli with the left eye, i.e. using the right brain hemisphere (similar to horses, Australian  magpies, chicks, toads and fish) but use the right eye, i.e. using the left hemisphere, for viewing familiar stimuli.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robins_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robins-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Temperament_and_emotions">Temperament and emotions</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CowPosture_20150612.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3485" data-file-width="3108" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/CowPosture_20150612.jpg/220px-CowPosture_20150612.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/CowPosture_20150612.jpg/330px-CowPosture_20150612.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/CowPosture_20150612.jpg/440px-CowPosture_20150612.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CowPosture_20150612.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ear postures of cows are studied as indicators of their emotional state and overall <a href="/wiki/Animal_welfare" title="Animal welfare">animal welfare</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In cattle, temperament can affect production traits such as carcass and meat quality or milk yield as well as affecting the animal's overall health and reproduction. Cattle temperament is defined as "the consistent behavioral and physiological difference observed between individuals in response to a stressor or environmental challenge and is used to describe the relatively stable difference in the behavioral predisposition of an animal, which can be related to psychobiological mechanisms".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brand2015_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brand2015-66">[66]</a></sup> Generally, cattle temperament is assumed to be multidimensional. Five underlying categories of temperament traits have been proposed:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reale2007_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reale2007-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>shyness-boldness</li>
<li>exploration-avoidance</li>
<li>activity</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aggression_in_cattle" title="Aggression in cattle">aggressiveness</a></li>
<li>sociability</li></ul>
<p>In a study on Holstein–Friesian heifers learning to press a panel to open a gate for access to a food reward, the researchers also recorded the heart rate and behavior of the heifers when moving along the race towards the food. When the heifers made clear improvements in learning, they had higher heart rates and tended to move more vigorously along the race. The researchers concluded this was an indication that cattle may react emotionally to their own learning improvement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hagen_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hagen-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Negative emotional states are associated with a bias toward negative (pessimistic) responses towards ambiguous cues in judgement tasks – as encapsulated in the question of <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_bias" title="Cognitive bias">"is the glass half empty or half full?"</a>.  After separation from their mothers, Holstein calves showed such a cognitive bias indicative of low mood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>  A similar study showed that after hot-iron disbudding (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dehorning" title="Dehorning">dehorning</a>), calves had a similar negative bias indicating that post-operative pain following this routine procedure results in a negative change in emotional state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neave_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neave-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>In studies of visual discrimination, the position of the ears has been used as an indicator of emotional state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coulon2011_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-Coulon2011-55">[55]</a></sup> When cattle are stressed other cattle can tell by the chemicals released in their urine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boissy1998_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boissy1998-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cattle are very <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_animal" title="Social animal">gregarious</a> and even short-term isolation is considered to cause severe psychological <a href="/wiki/Stress_(biology)" title="Stress (biology)">stress</a>.  When <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aubrac_(cattle)" title="Aubrac (cattle)">Aubrac</a> and Friesian heifers are isolated, they increase their vocalizations and experience increased <a href="/wiki/Heart_rate" title="Heart rate">heart rate</a> and plasma <a href="/wiki/Cortisol" title="Cortisol">cortisol</a> concentrations.  These physiological changes are greater in Aubracs.  When visual contact is re-instated, vocalisations rapidly decline, regardless of the familiarity of the returning cattle, however, heart rate decreases are greater if the returning cattle are familiar to the previously-isolated individual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>  Mirrors have been used to reduce stress in isolated cattle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Senses">Senses</span></h2>
<p>Cattle use all of the five widely recognized sensory modalities.  These can assist in some complex behavioural patterns, for example, in grazing behaviour.  Cattle eat mixed diets, but when given the opportunity, show a partial preference of approximately 70% clover and 30% grass. This preference has a diurnal pattern, with a stronger preference for clover in the morning, and the proportion of grass increasing towards the evening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vision">Vision</span></h3>
<p>Vision is the dominant sense in cattle and they obtain almost 50% of their information visually.
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adamczyk_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adamczyk-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cattle are a prey animal and to assist predator detection, their eyes are located on the sides of their head rather than the front.  This gives them a wide field of view of 330° but limits <a href="/wiki/Binocular_vision" title="Binocular vision">binocular vision</a> (and therefore <a href="/wiki/Stereopsis" title="Stereopsis">stereopsis</a>) to 30° to 50° compared to 140° in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coulon2011_55-3"><a href="#cite_note-Coulon2011-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2008_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2008-76">[76]</a></sup>  This means they have a blind spot directly behind them.  Cattle have good visual acuity (1/20)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coulon2011_55-4"><a href="#cite_note-Coulon2011-55">[55]</a></sup> but compared to humans, the visual accommodation of cattle is poor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adamczyk_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Adamczyk-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cattle have two kinds of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Color_receptors" title="Color receptors">color receptors</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cone_cells" title="Cone cells">cone cells</a> of their <a href="/wiki/Retina" title="Retina">retinas</a>.   This means that cattle are <a href="/wiki/Dichromacy" title="Dichromacy">dichromatic</a>, as are most other non-primate land mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> There are two to three rods per cone in the <a href="/wiki/Fovea_centralis" title="Fovea centralis">fovea centralis</a> but five to six near the optic papilla.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2008_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2008-76">[76]</a></sup> Cattle can distinguish long wavelength colors (yellow, orange and red) much better than the shorter wavelengths (blue, grey and green). Calves are able to discriminate between long (red) and short (blue) or medium (green) wavelengths, but have limited ability to discriminate between the short and medium.  They also approach handlers more quickly under red light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2001_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2001-79">[79]</a></sup> Whilst having good color sensitivity, it is not as good as humans or sheep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coulon2011_55-5"><a href="#cite_note-Coulon2011-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>A common misconception about cattle (particularly bulls) is that they are enraged by the color red (something provocative is often said to be "like a red flag to a bull"). This is a myth. In <a href="/wiki/Bullfighting" title="Bullfighting">bullfighting</a>, it is the movement of the red flag or cape that irritates the bull and incites it to charge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taste">Taste</span></h3>
<p>Cattle have a well-developed sense of taste and can distinguish the four primary tastes (sweet, salty, bitter and sour). They possess around 20,000 taste buds. The strength of taste perception depends on the individual's current food requirements. They avoid bitter-tasting foods (potentially toxic) and have a marked preference for sweet (high calorific value) and salty foods (<a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolyte</a> balance).  Their sensitivity to sour-tasting foods helps them to maintain optimal ruminal <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adamczyk_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-Adamczyk-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plants have low levels of sodium and cattle have developed the capacity of seeking salt by taste and smell.  If cattle become depleted of sodium salts, they show increased locomotion directed to searching for these.  To assist in their search, the olfactory and gustatory receptors able to detect minute amounts of sodium salts increase their sensitivity as biochemical disruption develops with sodium salt depletion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Audition">Audition</span></h3>
<p>Cattle hearing ranges from 23 <a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">Hz</a> to 35 kHz.  Their frequency of best sensitivity is 8 kHz and they have a lowest threshold of −21 <a href="/wiki/Decibel" title="Decibel">db</a> (re 20 μN/m<sup>−2</sup>), which means their hearing is more acute than horses (lowest threshold of 7 db).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heffner_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heffner-83">[83]</a></sup> Sound localization acuity thresholds are an average of 30°.  This means that cattle are less able to localise sounds compared to goats (18°), dogs (8°) and humans (0.8°).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heffner1992_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heffner1992-84">[84]</a></sup>  Because cattle have a broad foveal fields of view covering almost the entire horizon, they may not need very accurate locus information from their auditory systems to direct their gaze to a sound source.
</p><p>Vocalisations are an important mode of communication amongst cattle and can provide information on the age, sex, dominance status and reproductive status of the caller. Calves can recognize their mothers using vocalizations; vocal behaviour may play a role by indicating estrus and competitive display by bulls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watts2000_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watts2000-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Olfaction_and_gustation">Olfaction and gustation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_born_Frisian_red_white_calf.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1732" data-file-width="2309" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/New_born_Frisian_red_white_calf.jpg/250px-New_born_Frisian_red_white_calf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/New_born_Frisian_red_white_calf.jpg/375px-New_born_Frisian_red_white_calf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/New_born_Frisian_red_white_calf.jpg/500px-New_born_Frisian_red_white_calf.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_born_Frisian_red_white_calf.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Several senses are used in social relationships between cattle</div></div></div>
<p>Cattle have a range of odiferous glands over their body including <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Interdigital&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Interdigital (page does not exist)">interdigital</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Infraorbital_salivary_gland&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Infraorbital salivary gland (page does not exist)">infraorbital</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/inguinal" title="wikt:inguinal">inguinal</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sebaceous" title="Sebaceous">sebaceous</a> glands, indicating that olfaction probably plays a large role in their social life.  Both the primary olfactory system using the <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_bulb" title="Olfactory bulb">olfactory bulbs</a>, and the secondary olfactory system using the <a href="/wiki/Vomeronasal_organ" title="Vomeronasal organ">vomeronasal organ</a> are used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keeling_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keeling-86">[86]</a></sup>  This latter olfactory system is used in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flehmen" title="Flehmen">flehmen</a> response.  There is evidence that when cattle are stressed, this can be recognised by other cattle and this is communicated by alarm substances in the urine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boissy1998_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boissy1998-71">[71]</a></sup>  The odour of dog faeces induces behavioural changes prior to cattle feeding, whereas the odours of urine from either stressed or non-stressed conspecifics and blood have no effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Terlouw1998_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Terlouw1998-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the laboratory, cattle can be trained to recognise conspecific individuals using olfaction only.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keeling_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Keeling-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>In general, cattle use their sense of smell to "expand" on information detected by other sensory modalities.  However, in the case of social and reproductive behaviours, olfaction is a key source of information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adamczyk_75-3"><a href="#cite_note-Adamczyk-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Touch">Touch</span></h3>
<p>Cattle have tactile sensations detected mainly by <a href="/wiki/Mechanoreceptor" title="Mechanoreceptor">mechanoreceptors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thermoreceptor" title="Thermoreceptor">thermoreceptors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nociceptor" title="Nociceptor">nociceptors</a> in the skin and muzzle.  These are used most frequently when cattle explore their environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adamczyk_75-4"><a href="#cite_note-Adamczyk-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetoreception">Magnetoreception</span></h3>
<p>There is conflicting evidence for <a href="/wiki/Magnetoreception" title="Magnetoreception">magnetoreception</a> in cattle. One study reported that resting and grazing cattle tend to align their body axes in the geomagnetic North-South (N-S) direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>  In a follow-up study, cattle exposed to various magnetic fields directly beneath or in the vicinity of power lines trending in various magnetic directions exhibited distinct patterns of alignment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> However, in 2011, a group of <a href="/wiki/Czechs" title="Czechs">Czech</a> researchers reported their failed attempt to replicate the finding using <a href="/wiki/Google_Earth" title="Google Earth">Google Earth</a> images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavior">Behavior</span></h2>
<p>Under natural conditions, calves stay with their mother until weaning at 8 to 11 months.  Heifer and bull calves are equally attached to their mothers in the first few months of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnsen2015_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsen2015-91">[91]</a></sup>  Cattle are considered to be "hider" type animals, but in the artificial environment of small calving pens, close proximity between cow and calf is maintained by the mother at the first three calvings but this changes to being mediated by the calf after these.  Primiparous dams show a higher incidence of abnormal maternal behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
