codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;" data-title="VP9 (360P)" data-shorttitle="VP9 360P" data-transcodekey="360p.vp9.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" data-bandwidth="664408" data-framerate="50"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'><img alt="File:Calf suckling at a meadow near Vrachesh, Bulgaria.webm" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Calf_suckling_at_a_meadow_near_Vrachesh%2C_Bulgaria.webm/220px--Calf_suckling_at_a_meadow_near_Vrachesh%2C_Bulgaria.webm.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/dc/Calf_suckling_at_a_meadow_near_Vrachesh%2C_Bulgaria.webm" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Calf_suckling_at_a_meadow_near_Vrachesh,_Bulgaria.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Video of a calf suckling</div></div></div>
<p>Beef-calves reared on the range suckle an average of 5.0 times every 24 hours with an average total time of 46 min spent suckling. There is a diurnal rhythm in suckling activity with peaks between 05:00–07:00, 10:00–13:00 and 17:00–21:00.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Studies on the natural weaning of zebu cattle (<i>Bos indicus</i>) have shown that the cow weans her calves over a 2-week period, but after that, she continues to show strong affiliatory behavior with her offspring and preferentially chooses them for grooming and as grazing partners for at least 4–5 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproductive_behavior">Reproductive behavior</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cow_giving_birth,_in_Laos_(step_by_step).jpg"><img alt="Nine sequential photos showing the calf being born" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4630" data-file-width="6800" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Cow_giving_birth%2C_in_Laos_%28step_by_step%29.jpg/220px-Cow_giving_birth%2C_in_Laos_%28step_by_step%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Cow_giving_birth%2C_in_Laos_%28step_by_step%29.jpg/330px-Cow_giving_birth%2C_in_Laos_%28step_by_step%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Cow_giving_birth%2C_in_Laos_%28step_by_step%29.jpg/440px-Cow_giving_birth%2C_in_Laos_%28step_by_step%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cow_giving_birth,_in_Laos_(step_by_step).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A cow giving birth</div></div></div>
<p>Semi-wild <a href="/wiki/Highland_cattle" title="Highland cattle">Highland cattle</a> heifers first give birth at 2 or 3 years of age, and the timing of birth is synchronized with increases in natural food quality. Average calving interval is 391 days, and calving mortality within the first year of life is 5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reinhardt1986_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reinhardt1986-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dominance_and_leadership">Dominance and leadership</span></h3>
<p>One study showed that over a 4-year period, dominance relationships within a herd of semi-wild highland cattle were very firm. There were few overt aggressive conflicts and the majority of disputes were settled by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agonistic_behavior" title="Agonistic behavior">agonistic</a> (non-aggressive, competitive) behaviors that involved no physical contact between opponents (e.g. threatening and spontaneous withdrawing).  Such agonistic behavior reduces the risk of injury. Dominance status depended on age and sex, with older animals generally being dominant to young ones and males dominant to females. Young bulls gained superior dominance status over adult cows when they reached about 2 years of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reinhardt1986_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Reinhardt1986-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>As with many animal dominance hierarchies, dominance-associated aggressiveness does not correlate with rank position, but is closely related to rank distance between individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reinhardt1986_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-Reinhardt1986-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dominance is maintained in several ways.  Cattle often engage in mock fights where they test each other's strength in a non-aggressive way.  Licking is primarily performed by subordinates and received by dominant animals.  Mounting is a playful behavior shown by calves of both sexes and by bulls and sometimes by cows in estrus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> however, this is not a dominance related behavior as has been found in other species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reinhardt1986_95-3"><a href="#cite_note-Reinhardt1986-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>The horns of cattle are "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honest_signals" title="Honest signals">honest signals</a>" used in mate selection.  Furthermore, horned cattle attempt to keep greater distances between themselves and have fewer physical interactions than hornless cattle.  This leads to more stable social relationships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>In calves, the frequency of agonistic behavior decreases as space allowance increases, but this does not occur for changes in group size. However, in adult cattle, the number of agonistic encounters increases as the group size increases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grazing_behavior">Grazing behavior</span></h3>
<p>When grazing, cattle vary several aspects of their bite, i.e. tongue and jaw movements, depending on characteristics of the plant they are eating.  Bite area decreases with the density of the plants but increases with their height.  Bite area is determined by the sweep of the tongue; in one study observing 750-kilogram (1,650 lb) steers, bite area reached a maximum of approximately 170 cm<sup>2</sup> (30 sq in).  Bite depth increases with the height of the plants.  By adjusting their behavior, cattle obtain heavier bites in swards that are tall and sparse compared with short, dense swards of equal mass/area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>  Cattle adjust other aspects of their grazing behavior in relation to the available food; foraging velocity decreases and intake rate increases in areas of abundant palatable forage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cattle avoid grazing areas contaminated by the faeces of other cattle more strongly than they avoid areas contaminated by sheep,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> but they do not avoid pasture contaminated by rabbit faeces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Bovine_genome" title="Bovine genome">Bovine genome</a></div>
<p>In the 24 April 2009, edition of the journal <i>Science</i>, a team of researchers led by the National Institutes of Health and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">US Department of Agriculture</a> reported having mapped the <a href="/wiki/Bovine_genome" title="Bovine genome">bovine genome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cattle_genome_yahoo_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cattle_genome_yahoo-103">[103]</a></sup>  The scientists found cattle have about 22,000 genes, and 80% of their genes are shared with humans, and they share about 1000 genes with dogs and rodents, but are not found in humans. Using this bovine "HapMap", researchers can track the differences between the breeds that affect the quality of meat and milk yields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>Behavioral traits of cattle can be as <a href="/wiki/Heritability" title="Heritability">heritable</a> as some production traits, and often, the two can be related.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> The heritability of fear varies markedly in cattle from low (0.1) to high (0.53); such high variation is also found in pigs and sheep,  probably due to differences in the methods used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jensen2009_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jensen2009-106">[106]</a></sup>  The heritability of temperament (response to isolation during handling) has been calculated as 0.36 and 0.46 for <a href="/wiki/Habituation" title="Habituation">habituation</a> to handling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>  Rangeland assessments show that the heritability of aggressiveness in cattle is around 0.36.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantitative_trait_loci" title="Quantitative trait loci">Quantitative trait loci</a> (QTLs) have been found for a range of production and behavioral characteristics for both dairy and beef cattle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Friedrich_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Friedrich-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestication_and_husbandry">Domestication and husbandry</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Texas_Longhorn.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="188" data-file-width="271" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Texas_Longhorn.jpg/220px-Texas_Longhorn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1e/Texas_Longhorn.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Texas_Longhorn.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Texas_Longhorn" title="Texas Longhorn">Texas Longhorns</a> are a US breed</div></div></div>
<p>Cattle occupy a unique role in <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_world" title="History of the world">human history</a>, having been domesticated since at least the early <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">neolithic</a> age.
</p><p>Archeozoological and genetic data indicate that cattle were first domesticated from wild <a href="/wiki/Aurochs" title="Aurochs">aurochs</a> (<i>Bos primigenius</i>) approximately 10,500 years ago. There were two major areas of domestication: one in the area that is now Turkey, giving rise to the taurine line, and a second in the area that is now Pakistan, resulting in the indicine line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McTavish_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-McTavish-110">[110]</a></sup>  Modern mitochondrial DNA variation indicates the taurine line may have arisen from as few as 80 <a href="/wiki/Aurochs" title="Aurochs">aurochs</a> tamed in the upper reaches of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> near the villages of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%87ay%C3%B6n%C3%BC_Tepesi" title="Çayönü Tepesi">Çayönü Tepesi</a> in southeastern Turkey and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dja%27de_el-Mughara&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dja'de el-Mughara (page does not exist)">Dja'de el-Mughara</a> in northern <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HowNowCow_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-HowNowCow-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although European cattle are largely descended from the taurine lineage, gene flow from African cattle (partially of indicine origin) contributed substantial genomic components to both southern European cattle breeds and their New World descendants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McTavish_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-McTavish-110">[110]</a></sup>  A study on 134 breeds showed that modern taurine cattle originated from Africa, Asia, North and South America, Australia, and Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Decker_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Decker-111">[111]</a></sup>  Some researchers have suggested that African taurine cattle are derived from a third independent domestication from North African aurochsen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McTavish_110-2"><a href="#cite_note-McTavish-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Usage_as_money">Usage as money</span></h3>
<p>As early as 9000 BC both grain and cattle were used as money or as <i>barter</i> (Davies) (the <i><a href="/wiki/Ohalo" title="Ohalo">first grain remains</a></i> found, considered to be evidence of pre-agricultural practice date to 17,000 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies2002_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies2002-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_Soto_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-de_Soto-114">[114]</a></sup> Some evidence also exists to suggest that other animals, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Camels" title="Camels">camels</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goats" title="Goats">goats</a>, may have been used as currency in some parts of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> One of the advantages of using cattle as currency is that it allows the seller to set a fixed price. It even created the standard pricing. For example, two chickens were traded for one cow as cows were deemed to be more valuable than chickens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies2002_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davies2002-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_husbandry">Modern husbandry</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cattle_inspected_for_ticks.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1780" data-file-width="2700" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Cattle_inspected_for_ticks.jpg/220px-Cattle_inspected_for_ticks.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Cattle_inspected_for_ticks.jpg/330px-Cattle_inspected_for_ticks.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Cattle_inspected_for_ticks.jpg/440px-Cattle_inspected_for_ticks.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cattle_inspected_for_ticks.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This Hereford is being inspected for <a href="/wiki/Tick" title="Tick">ticks</a>; cattle are often restrained or confined in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cattle_crush" title="Cattle crush">cattle crushes</a> (squeeze chutes) when given medical attention.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anneau_anti_tetee_P1190486.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Anneau_anti_tetee_P1190486.jpg/220px-Anneau_anti_tetee_P1190486.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Anneau_anti_tetee_P1190486.jpg/330px-Anneau_anti_tetee_P1190486.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Anneau_anti_tetee_P1190486.jpg/440px-Anneau_anti_tetee_P1190486.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anneau_anti_tetee_P1190486.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This young bovine has a <a href="/wiki/Nose_ring_(animal)" title="Nose ring (animal)">nose ring</a> to prevent it from suckling, which is usually to assist in <a href="/wiki/Weaning" title="Weaning">weaning</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Cattle are often raised by allowing herds to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grass_fed_beef" title="Grass fed beef">graze on the grasses</a> of large tracts of <a href="/wiki/Rangeland" title="Rangeland">rangeland</a>. Raising cattle in this manner allows the use of land that might be unsuitable for growing crops. The most common interactions with cattle involve daily <a href="/wiki/Cattle_feeding" title="Cattle feeding">feeding</a>, cleaning and <a href="/wiki/Milking" title="Milking">milking</a>. Many routine husbandry practices involve <a href="/wiki/Ear_tag" title="Ear tag">ear tagging</a>, <a href="/wiki/Livestock_dehorning" title="Livestock dehorning">dehorning</a>, loading, <a href="/wiki/Veterinary_surgery" title="Veterinary surgery">medical operations</a>, vaccinations and <a href="/wiki/Cloven_hoof" title="Cloven hoof">hoof</a> care, as well as training for agricultural shows and preparations. Also, some <a href="/wiki/Cultural_identity" title="Cultural identity">cultural differences</a> occur in working with cattle; the cattle husbandry of Fulani men rests on <a href="/wiki/Ethology" title="Ethology">behavioural techniques</a>, whereas in Europe, cattle are controlled primarily by physical means, such as <a href="/wiki/Fence" title="Fence">fences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>  Breeders use cattle husbandry to reduce <a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_bovis" title="Mycobacterium bovis"><i>M. bovis</i> infection</a> susceptibility by <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selective breeding</a> and maintaining herd health to avoid concurrent disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cattle are farmed for beef, veal, dairy, and leather, and they are less commonly used for <a href="/wiki/Conservation_grazing" title="Conservation grazing">conservation grazing</a>, simply to maintain grassland for wildlife – for example, in <a href="/wiki/Epping_Forest" title="Epping Forest">Epping Forest</a>, England. They are often used in some of the most wild places for livestock. Depending on the breed, cattle can survive on hill grazing, heaths, marshes, moors and semidesert. Modern cattle are more commercial than older breeds and, having become more specialized, are less versatile. For this reason, many smaller farmers still favor old breeds, such as the <a href="/wiki/Jersey_cattle" title="Jersey cattle">Jersey</a> dairy breed.
In Portugal, Spain, <a href="/wiki/Southern_France" title="Southern France">southern France</a> and some Latin American countries, bulls are used in the activity of <a href="/wiki/Bullfighting" title="Bullfighting">bullfighting</a>;  <i><a href="/wiki/Jallikattu" title="Jallikattu">Jallikattu</a></i> in India is a bull taming sport radically different from European bullfighting, humans are unarmed and bulls are not killed. In many other countries bullfighting is illegal. Other activities such as <a href="/wiki/Bull_riding" title="Bull riding">bull riding</a> are seen as part of a <a href="/wiki/Rodeo" title="Rodeo">rodeo</a>, especially in North America. <a href="/wiki/Bull-leaping" title="Bull-leaping">Bull-leaping</a>, a central ritual in <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan</a> culture (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacred_Bull" title="Sacred Bull">Sacred Bull</a>), still exists in southwestern France.  In modern times, cattle are also entered into <a href="/wiki/Cattle_judging" title="Cattle judging">agricultural competitions</a>. These competitions can involve live cattle or cattle carcases in hoof and hook events.
</p><p>In terms of food intake by humans, consumption of cattle is less efficient than of grain or vegetables with regard to <a href="/wiki/Land_use" title="Land use">land use</a>, and hence cattle grazing consumes more area than such other agricultural production when raised on grains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson-118">[118]</a></sup> Nonetheless, cattle and other forms of domesticated animals can sometimes help to use plant resources in areas not easily amenable to other forms of agriculture. Bulls are sometimes used as <a href="/wiki/Guard_dog" title="Guard dog">guard animals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sleep">Sleep</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sleep_in_non-human_animals" title="Sleep in non-human animals">Sleep in non-human animals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cow_tipping" title="Cow tipping">Cow tipping</a></div>
<p>The average sleep time of a domestic cow is about 4 hours a day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>  Cattle do have a <a href="/wiki/Stay_apparatus" title="Stay apparatus">stay apparatus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> but do not sleep standing up,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> they lie down to sleep deeply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-collins_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-collins-124">[124]</a></sup>  In spite of the <a href="/wiki/Urban_legend" title="Urban legend">urban legend</a>, cows cannot be tipped over by people pushing on them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haines_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haines-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cow_female_black_white.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1680" data-file-width="2190" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Cow_female_black_white.jpg/220px-Cow_female_black_white.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Cow_female_black_white.jpg/330px-Cow_female_black_white.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Cow_female_black_white.jpg/440px-Cow_female_black_white.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cow_female_black_white.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Holstein_Friesian_cattle" title="Holstein Friesian cattle">Holstein cattle</a> are the primary dairy breed, bred for high milk production.</div></div></div>
<p>The meat of adult cattle is known as <a href="/wiki/Beef" title="Beef">beef</a>, and that of <a href="/wiki/Calf" title="Calf">calves</a> is <a href="/wiki/Veal" title="Veal">veal</a>.  Other animal parts are also used as food products, including blood, <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxtail" title="Oxtail">oxtail</a>.  Cattle also produce <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dairy_cattle" title="Dairy cattle">dairy cattle</a> are specifically bred to produce the large quantities of milk processed and sold for human consumption. Cattle today are the basis of a multibillion-dollar industry worldwide. The <a href="/wiki/International_trade" title="International trade">international trade</a> in beef for 2000 was over $30 billion and represented only 23% of world beef production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> The production of milk, which is also made into cheese, butter, <a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">yogurt</a>, and other dairy products, is comparable in economic size to beef production, and provides an important part of the food supply for many of the world's people. Cattle hides, used for leather to make shoes, <a href="/wiki/Couch" title="Couch">couches</a> and clothing, are another widespread product.  Cattle remain broadly used as draft animals in many <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing countries</a>, such as India. Cattle are also used in some sporting games, including <a href="/wiki/Rodeo" title="Rodeo">rodeo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bullfighting" title="Bullfighting">bullfighting</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cattle_meat_production">Cattle meat production</span></h3>
<table class="wikitable sortable">

</p><p>About half the world's meat comes from cattle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dairy">Dairy</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Dairy_cattle" title="Dairy cattle">Dairy cattle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dairy_farming" title="Dairy farming">Dairy farming</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dairy" title="Dairy">Dairy</a></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hand_milking_a_cow_at_Cobbes_Farm_Museum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="900" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Hand_milking_a_cow_at_Cobbes_Farm_Museum.jpg/170px-Hand_milking_a_cow_at_Cobbes_Farm_Museum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Hand_milking_a_cow_at_Cobbes_Farm_Museum.jpg/255px-Hand_milking_a_cow_at_Cobbes_Farm_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Hand_milking_a_cow_at_Cobbes_Farm_Museum.jpg/340px-Hand_milking_a_cow_at_Cobbes_Farm_Museum.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hand_milking_a_cow_at_Cobbes_Farm_Museum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dairy_farming" title="Dairy farming">Dairy farming</a> and the milking of cattle was once performed largely by hand, but is now <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milking_machine" title="Milking machine">usually replaced by machine</a></div></div></div>
<p>Certain breeds of cattle, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holstein-Friesian" title="Holstein-Friesian">Holstein-Friesian</a>, are used to produce <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ukcows_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-ukcows-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-130">[130]</a></sup> which can be processed into dairy products such as milk, <a href="/wiki/Cheese" title="Cheese">cheese</a> or <a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">yogurt</a>. Dairy cattle are usually kept on specialized dairy farms designed for milk production. Most cows are milked twice per day, with milk processed at a dairy, which may be onsite at the farm or the milk may be shipped to a dairy plant for eventual sale of a dairy product.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dairy_science_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-dairy_science-131">[131]</a></sup> For dairy cattle to continue producing milk, they must give birth to one calf per year. If the calf is male, it generally is slaughtered at a young age to produce <a href="/wiki/Veal" title="Veal">veal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> They will continue to produce milk until three weeks before birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-130">[130]</a></sup> Over the last fifty years, dairy farming has become more intensive to increase the yield of milk produced by each cow. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holstein-Friesian" title="Holstein-Friesian">Holstein-Friesian</a> is the breed of dairy cow most common in the UK, Europe and the United States. It has been bred selectively to produce the highest yields of milk of any cow. Around 22 litres per day is average in the UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ukcows_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-ukcows-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_130-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hides">Hides</span></h3>
<p>Most cattle are not kept solely for hides, which are usually a by-product of beef production. Hides are most commonly used for leather which can be made into a variety of product including shoes. In 2012 India was the world's largest producer of cattle hides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Feral_cattle">Feral cattle</span></h2>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feral_organism" title="Feral organism">Feral</a> cattle are defined as being 'cattle that are not domesticated or cultivated'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Populations of feral cattle are known to come from and exist in: <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_of_America" title="United States of America">United States of America</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> and many islands, including <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_Islands" title="Hawaiian Islands">Hawaii</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galapagos" title="Galapagos">Galapagos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Juan_Fern%C3%A1ndez_Islands" title="Juan Fernández Islands">Juan Fernández Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a> (<a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a>), <a href="/wiki/Tristan_da_Cunha" title="Tristan da Cunha">Tristan da Cunha</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C3%8Ele_Amsterdam" title="Île Amsterdam">Île Amsterdam</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSW3Bostaurus_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSW3Bostaurus-136">[136]</a></sup>  two islands of <a href="/wiki/Kuchinoshima" title="Kuchinoshima">Kuchinoshima</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> and Kazura Island next to <a href="/wiki/Naru_Island" title="Naru Island">Naru Island</a> in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chillingham_cattle" title="Chillingham cattle">Chillingham cattle</a> is sometimes regarded as a feral breed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aleutian_wild_cattle" title="Aleutian wild cattle">Aleutian wild cattles</a> can be found on <a href="/wiki/Aleutian_Islands" title="Aleutian Islands">Aleutian Islands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> The "Kinmen cattle" which is dominantly found on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kinmen_Island" title="Kinmen Island">Kinmen Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> is mostly domesticated while smaller portion of the population is believed to live in the wild due to accidental releases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other notable examples include cattles vicinity to Hong Kong (in the <a href="/wiki/Shing_Mun_Country_Park" title="Shing Mun Country Park">Shing Mun Country Park</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> among <a href="/wiki/Sai_Kung_District" title="Sai Kung District">Sai Kung District</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Lantau_Island" title="Lantau Island">Lantau Island</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> and on <a href="/wiki/Grass_Island,_Hong_Kong" title="Grass Island, Hong Kong">Grass Island</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>), and semi-feral animals in <a href="/wiki/Yangmingshan" title="Yangmingshan">Yangmingshan</a>, Taiwan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_effects_of_meat_production" title="Environmental effects of meat production">Environmental effects of meat production</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CSIRO_ScienceImage_1672_Cattle_in_dry_landscape.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2657" data-file-width="1835" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/CSIRO_ScienceImage_1672_Cattle_in_dry_landscape.jpg/170px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_1672_Cattle_in_dry_landscape.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/CSIRO_ScienceImage_1672_Cattle_in_dry_landscape.jpg/255px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_1672_Cattle_in_dry_landscape.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/CSIRO_ScienceImage_1672_Cattle_in_dry_landscape.jpg/340px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_1672_Cattle_in_dry_landscape.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CSIRO_ScienceImage_1672_Cattle_in_dry_landscape.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cattle in dry landscape north of <a href="/wiki/Alice_Springs" title="Alice Springs">Alice Springs</a>, Australia (CSIRO)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2013-06-28_15_48_26_Cattle_along_Deeth-Charleston_Road_(Elko_County_Route_747)_at_the_Bruneau_River,_about_38.6_miles_north_of_Deeth_in_Elko_County,_Nevada.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1725" data-file-width="2913" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/2013-06-28_15_48_26_Cattle_along_Deeth-Charleston_Road_%28Elko_County_Route_747%29_at_the_Bruneau_River%2C_about_38.6_miles_north_of_Deeth_in_Elko_County%2C_Nevada.jpg/220px-2013-06-28_15_48_26_Cattle_along_Deeth-Charleston_Road_%28Elko_County_Route_747%29_at_the_Bruneau_River%2C_about_38.6_miles_north_of_Deeth_in_Elko_County%2C_Nevada.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/2013-06-28_15_48_26_Cattle_along_Deeth-Charleston_Road_%28Elko_County_Route_747%29_at_the_Bruneau_River%2C_about_38.6_miles_north_of_Deeth_in_Elko_County%2C_Nevada.jpg/330px-2013-06-28_15_48_26_Cattle_along_Deeth-Charleston_Road_%28Elko_County_Route_747%29_at_the_Bruneau_River%2C_about_38.6_miles_north_of_Deeth_in_Elko_County%2C_Nevada.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/2013-06-28_15_48_26_Cattle_along_Deeth-Charleston_Road_%28Elko_County_Route_747%29_at_the_Bruneau_River%2C_about_38.6_miles_north_of_Deeth_in_Elko_County%2C_Nevada.jpg/440px-2013-06-28_15_48_26_Cattle_along_Deeth-Charleston_Road_%28Elko_County_Route_747%29_at_the_Bruneau_River%2C_about_38.6_miles_north_of_Deeth_in_Elko_County%2C_Nevada.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2013-06-28_15_48_26_Cattle_along_Deeth-Charleston_Road_(Elko_County_Route_747)_at_the_Bruneau_River,_about_38.6_miles_north_of_Deeth_in_Elko_County,_Nevada.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cattle near the <a href="/wiki/Bruneau_River" title="Bruneau River">Bruneau River</a> in <a href="/wiki/Elko_County,_Nevada" title="Elko County, Nevada">Elko County, Nevada</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gut_flora" title="Gut flora">Gut flora</a> in cattle include <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">methanogens</a> that <a href="/wiki/Methanogenesis" title="Methanogenesis">produce methane</a> as a byproduct of <a href="/wiki/Enteric_fermentation" title="Enteric fermentation">enteric fermentation</a>, which cattle belch out. The same volume of <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_methane" title="Atmospheric methane">atmospheric methane</a> has a higher <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">global warming potential</a> than <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">atmospheric carbon dioxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gunnar2013_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gunnar2013-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Methane belching from cattle can be reduced with genetic selection, immunization, rumen defaunation, diet modification and grazing management, among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>A report from the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> (FAO) states that the livestock sector is "responsible for 18% of greenhouse gas emissions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steinfeld_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steinfeld-153">[153]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IPCC" title="IPCC">IPCC</a> estimates that Cattle and other livestock emit about 80 to 93 Megatonnes of methane per year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> accounting for an estimated 37% of anthropogenic methane emissions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steinfeld_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-Steinfeld-153">[153]</a></sup> and additional methane is produced by anaerobic fermentation of manure in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manure_lagoon" title="Manure lagoon">manure lagoons</a> and other manure storage structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> The net change in atmospheric methane content was recently about 1 Megatonne per year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup>  and in some recent years there has been no increase in atmospheric methane content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup>  While cattle fed forage actually produce more methane than grain-fed cattle, the increase may be offset by the increased carbon recapture of pastures, which recapture three times the CO<sub>2</sub> of cropland used for grain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCS_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-UCS-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the cited changes suggested to reduce greenhouse gas emissions is intensification of the livestock industry, since intensification leads to less land for a given level of production. This assertion is supported by studies of the US beef production system, suggesting practices prevailing in 2007 involved 8.6% less fossil fuel use, 16.3% less greenhouse gas emissions, 12.1% less water use, and 33.0% less land use, per unit mass of beef produced, than those used in 1977.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup>  The analysis took into account not only practices in <a href="/wiki/Feedlot" title="Feedlot">feedlots</a>, but also feed production (with less feed needed in more intensive production systems), forage-based cow-calf operations and back-grounding before cattle enter a feedlot (with more beef produced per head of cattle from those sources, in more intensive systems), and beef from animals derived from the dairy industry.
</p><p>The number of American cattle kept in confined feedlot conditions fluctuates. From 1 January 2002 through 1 January 2012, there was no significant overall upward or downward trend in the number of US cattle on feed for slaughter, which averaged about 14.046 million head over that period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDA201176_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-USDA201176-160">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDA2012Catt_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-USDA2012Catt-161">[161]</a></sup>  Previously, the number had increased; it was 12.453 million in 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> Cattle on feed (for slaughter) numbered about 14.121 million on 1 January 2012, i.e. about 15.5% of the estimated inventory of 90.8 million US cattle (including calves) on that date.  Of the 14.121 million, US cattle on feed (for slaughter) in operations with 1000 head or more were estimated to number 11.9 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDA2012Catt_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-USDA2012Catt-161">[161]</a></sup> Cattle feedlots in this size category correspond to the regulatory definition of "large" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concentrated_animal_feeding_operations" title="Concentrated animal feeding operations">concentrated animal feeding operations</a> (CAFOs) for cattle other than mature dairy cows or veal calves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40CFR122_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-40CFR122-163">[163]</a></sup>  Significant numbers of dairy, as well as beef cattle, are confined in CAFOs, defined as "new and existing operations which stable or confine and feed or maintain for a total of 45 days or more in any 12-month period more than the number of animals specified"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> where "[c]rops, vegetation, forage growth, or post-harvest residues are not sustained in the normal growing season over any portion of the lot or facility."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup>  They may be designated as small, medium and large. Such designation of cattle CAFOs is according to cattle type (mature dairy cows, veal calves or other) and cattle numbers, but medium CAFOs are so designated only if they meet certain discharge criteria, and small CAFOs are designated only on a case-by-case basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-size_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-size-166">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p>A CAFO that discharges pollutants is required to obtain a permit, which requires a plan to manage nutrient runoff, manure, chemicals, contaminants, and other wastewater pursuant to the US <a href="/wiki/Clean_Water_Act" title="Clean Water Act">Clean Water Act</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> The regulations involving CAFO permitting have been extensively litigated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup>
Commonly, CAFO wastewater and manure nutrients are applied to land at agronomic rates for use by forages or crops, and it is often assumed that various constituents of wastewater and manure, e.g. organic contaminants and pathogens, will be retained, inactivated or degraded on the land with application at such rates; however, additional evidence is needed to test reliability of such assumptions
.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup>  Concerns raised by opponents of CAFOs have included risks of contaminated water due to <a href="/wiki/Feedlot" title="Feedlot">feedlot</a> runoff,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koelsch_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koelsch-170">[170]</a></sup> soil erosion, human and animal exposure to toxic chemicals, development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistant_bacteria" title="Antibiotic resistant bacteria">antibiotic resistant bacteria</a> and an increase in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E._coli" title="E. coli">E. coli</a></i> contamination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup>  While research suggests some of these impacts can be mitigated by developing wastewater treatment systems<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koelsch_170-1"><a href="#cite_note-Koelsch-170">[170]</a></sup> and planting cover crops in larger setback zones,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_Concerned_Scientists" title="Union of Concerned Scientists">Union of Concerned Scientists</a> released a report in 2008 concluding that CAFOs are generally unsustainable and <a href="/wiki/Cost_externalizing" title="Cost externalizing">externalize costs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCS_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-UCS-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>An estimated 935,000 cattle operations were operating in the USA in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDA201171_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-USDA201171-173">[173]</a></sup> In 2001, the US Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) tallied 5,990 cattle CAFOs then regulated, consisting of beef (2,200), dairy (3,150), heifer (620) and veal operations (20).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> Since that time, the EPA has established CAFOs as an enforcement priority.  EPA enforcement highlights for fiscal year 2010 indicated enforcement actions against 12 cattle CAFOs for violations that included failures to obtain a permit, failures to meet the terms of a permit, and discharges of contaminated water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cattle_at_Velika_Planina,_Slovenia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Cattle_at_Velika_Planina%2C_Slovenia.jpg/300px-Cattle_at_Velika_Planina%2C_Slovenia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Cattle_at_Velika_Planina%2C_Slovenia.jpg/450px-Cattle_at_Velika_Planina%2C_Slovenia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Cattle_at_Velika_Planina%2C_Slovenia.jpg/600px-Cattle_at_Velika_Planina%2C_Slovenia.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cattle_at_Velika_Planina,_Slovenia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cattle grazing in a high-elevation environment at the <a href="/wiki/Big_Pasture_Plateau" title="Big Pasture Plateau">Big Pasture Plateau</a>, Slovenia</div></div></div>
<p>Another concern is <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">manure</a>, which if not well-managed, can lead to adverse environmental consequences. However, manure also is a valuable source of nutrients and organic matter when used as a fertilizer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup>  Manure was used as a fertilizer on about 15.8 million acres of US cropland in 2006, with manure from cattle accounting for nearly 70% of manure applications to soybeans and about 80% or more of manure applications to corn, wheat, barley, oats and sorghum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup>  Substitution of manure for synthetic fertilizers in crop production can be environmentally significant, as between 43 and 88 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megajoule" title="Megajoule">megajoules</a> of fossil fuel energy would be used per kg of nitrogen in manufacture of synthetic nitrogenous fertilizers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup>
</p><p>Grazing by cattle at low intensities can create a favourable environment for native herbs and <a href="/wiki/Forb" title="Forb">forbs</a>; in many world regions, though, cattle are reducing <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Overgrazing" title="Overgrazing">overgrazing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup>  A survey of refuge managers on 123 National Wildlife Refuges in the US tallied 86 species of wildlife considered positively affected and 82 considered negatively affected by refuge cattle grazing or haying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> Proper management of pastures, notably <a href="/wiki/Managed_intensive_rotational_grazing" title="Managed intensive rotational grazing">managed intensive rotational grazing</a> and grazing at low intensities can lead to less use of fossil fuel energy, increased recapture of carbon dioxide, fewer ammonia emissions into the atmosphere, reduced soil erosion, better air quality, and less water pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCS_158-2"><a href="#cite_note-UCS-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Cattle conservation and preservation is important for maintaining ecology of the rural areas and villages which in turn is crucial for nature conservation. <b>Govidyapeetham, a cattle conservation institute</b> by Aniruddha’s Institute of Gramin Vikas (Rural Development) situated in Karjat city in the state of Maharashtra, India is dedicated for research based reproduction of cows using modern technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aniruddhafoundation.com_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-aniruddhafoundation.com-181">[181]</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Govidyapeetham1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="909" data-file-width="750" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Govidyapeetham1.jpg/220px-Govidyapeetham1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Govidyapeetham1.jpg/330px-Govidyapeetham1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Govidyapeetham1.jpg/440px-Govidyapeetham1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Govidyapeetham1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Govidyapeetham, Cattle Conservation Institute, India</div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h2>
<p>The veterinary discipline dealing with cattle and cattle diseases (bovine veterinary) is called buiatrics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> Veterinarians and professionals working on cattle health issues are pooled in the World Association for Buiatrics, founded in 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> National associations and affiliates also exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cattle diseases were in the center of attention in the 1980s and 1990s when the <a href="/wiki/Bovine_spongiform_encephalopathy" title="Bovine spongiform encephalopathy">Bovine spongiform encephalopathy</a> (BSE), also known as mad cow disease, was of concern.  Cattle might catch and develop various other diseases, like <a href="/wiki/Blackleg_(disease)" title="Blackleg (disease)">blackleg</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bluetongue" title="Bluetongue">bluetongue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foot_rot" title="Foot rot">foot rot</a> too.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p>In most states, as cattle health is not only a veterinarian issue, but also a public health issue, <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a> and <a href="/wiki/Food_safety" title="Food safety">food safety</a> standards and farming regulations directly affect the daily work of farmers who keep cattle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> However, said rules change frequently and are often debated. For instance, in the U.K., it was proposed in 2011 that milk from <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>-infected cattle should be allowed to enter the food chain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> Internal food safety regulations might affect a country's trade policy as well. For example, the United States has just reviewed its beef import rules according to the "mad cow standards"; while Mexico forbids the entry of cattle who are older than 30 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cow_urine" title="Cow urine">Cow urine</a> is commonly used in India for internal medical purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup>  It is distilled and then consumed by patients seeking treatment for a wide variety of illnesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> At present, no conclusive medical evidence shows this has any effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> However, an Indian medicine containing cow urine has already obtained U.S. patents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Digital_dermatitis" title="Digital dermatitis">Digital dermatitis</a> is caused by the bacteria from the genus Treponema. It differs from foot rot and can appear under unsanitary conditions such as poor hygiene or inadequate hoof trimming, among other causes. It primarily affects dairy cattle and has been known to lower the quantity of milk produced, however the milk quality remains unaffected. Cattle are also susceptible to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ringworm" title="Ringworm">ringworm</a> caused by the fungus, <i><a href="/wiki/Trichophyton_verrucosum" title="Trichophyton verrucosum">Trichophyton verrucosum</a></i>, a contagious skin disease which may be transferred to humans exposed to infected cows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beneke1996_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-beneke1996-196">[196]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_vaccae" title="Mycobacterium vaccae">Mycobacterium vaccae</a></i> is a non pathogenic, possibly even beneficial bacteria, that is seen naturally in soil;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-immune_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-immune-197">[197]</a></sup> that was first isolated from cow dung.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dung_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-dung-198">[198]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_of_high_stocking_density">Effect of high stocking density</span></h3>
<p>Stocking density refers to the number of animals within a specified area. When stocking density reaches high levels, the behavioural needs of the animals may not be met.  This can negatively influence health, welfare and production performance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p><p>The effect of overstocking in cows can have a negative effect on <a href="/wiki/Dairy" title="Dairy">milk production</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">reproduction</a> rates which are two very important traits for dairy farmers. Overcrowding of cows in barns has been found to reduced feeding, resting and rumination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_199-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-199">[199]</a></sup> Although they consume the same amount of dry matter within the span of a day, they consume the food at a much more rapid rate, and this behaviour in cows can lead to further complications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> The feeding behaviour of cows during their post-milking period is very important as it has been proven that the longer animals can eat after milking, the longer they will be standing up and therefore causing less contamination to the teat ends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-201">[201]</a></sup> This is necessary to reduce the risk of mastitis as infection has been shown to increase the chances of embryonic loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> Sufficient rest is important for dairy cows because it is during this period that their resting blood flow increases up to 50%, this is directly proportionate to milk production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_201-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-201">[201]</a></sup> Each additional hour of rest can be seen to translate to 2 to 3.5 more pounds of milk per cow daily. Stocking densities of anything over 120% have been shown to decrease the amount of time cows spend lying down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cortisol" title="Cortisol">Cortisol</a> is an important stress hormone; its plasma concentrations increase greatly when subjected to high levels of stress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sjaasted_O.V._2010_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sjaasted_O.V._2010-204">[204]</a></sup>  Increased concentration levels of cortisol have been associated with significant increases in gonadotrophin levels and lowered progestin levels. Reduction of stress is important in the reproductive state of cows as an increase in <a href="/wiki/Gonadotropin" title="Gonadotropin">gonadotrophin</a> and lowered <a href="/wiki/Progesterone" title="Progesterone">progesterone</a> levels may impinge on the <a href="/wiki/Ovulation" title="Ovulation">ovulatory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Luteal_phase" title="Luteal phase">lutenization</a> process and to reduce the chances of successful implantation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> A high cortisol level will also stimulate the degradation of fats and proteins which may make it difficult for the animal to sustain its pregnancy if implanted successfully.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sjaasted_O.V._2010_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sjaasted_O.V._2010-204">[204]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxen">Oxen</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ox" title="Ox">Ox</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:India.Mumbai.04.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/India.Mumbai.04.jpg/220px-India.Mumbai.04.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/India.Mumbai.04.jpg/330px-India.Mumbai.04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/India.Mumbai.04.jpg/440px-India.Mumbai.04.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:India.Mumbai.04.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Draft <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zebus" title="Zebus">Zebus</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">Mumbai</a>, Maharashtra, India</div></div></div>
<p><b>Oxen</b> (singular <b>ox</b>) are cattle trained as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Draft_animal" title="Draft animal">draft animals</a>. Often they are adult, <a href="/wiki/Castration" title="Castration">castrated</a> males of larger breeds, although females and bulls are also used in some areas. Usually, an <a href="/wiki/Ox" title="Ox">ox</a> is over four years old due to the need for training and to allow it to grow to full size. Oxen are used for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plowing" title="Plowing">plowing</a>, transport, hauling cargo, grain-grinding by trampling or by powering machines, <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> by powering pumps, and <a href="/wiki/Wagon" title="Wagon">wagon</a> drawing. Oxen were commonly used to skid logs in forests, and sometimes still are, in low-impact, select-cut logging. Oxen are most often used in teams of two, paired, for light work such as <a href="/wiki/Cart" title="Cart">carting</a>, with additional pairs added when more power is required, sometimes up to a total of 20 or more.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg/220px-Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg/330px-Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg/440px-Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Traditional_Farming_Methods_and_Equipments.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oxen used in Plowing</div></div></div>
<p>An ox is a mature bovine which has learned to respond appropriately to a <a href="/wiki/Teamster" title="Teamster">teamster</a>'s signals. These signals are given by verbal commands or by noise (whip cracks). Verbal commands vary according to dialect and local tradition.  In one tradition in North America, the commands are:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul><li>"Back up": go backwards</li>
<li>"Gee": turn right</li>
<li>"Get up": walk forward</li>
<li>"Haw": turn left</li>
<li>"Whoa": stop</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sixten.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1350" data-file-width="1578" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Sixten.jpg/220px-Sixten.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Sixten.jpg/330px-Sixten.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Sixten.jpg/440px-Sixten.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sixten.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Riding an ox in <a href="/wiki/Hova,_Sweden" title="Hova, Sweden">Hova, Sweden</a></div></div></div>
<p>Oxen can pull harder and longer than horses. Though not as fast as horses, they are less prone to injury because they are more sure-footed.
</p><p>Many oxen are used worldwide, especially in <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing countries</a>.  About 11.3 million draft oxen are used in sub-Saharan Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup>  In India, the number of draft cattle in 1998 was estimated at 65.7 million head.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> About half the world's crop production is thought to depend on land preparation (such as plowing) made possible by animal traction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OftheOX.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="427" data-file-width="599" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/OftheOX.jpg/220px-OftheOX.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/OftheOX.jpg/330px-OftheOX.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/OftheOX.jpg/440px-OftheOX.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OftheOX.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The "Ure-Ox" (<a href="/wiki/Aurochs" title="Aurochs">Aurochs</a>) by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Topsell" title="Edward Topsell">Edward Topsell</a>, 1658</div></div></div>
<h2><span id="Religion.2C_traditions_and_folklore"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion,_traditions_and_folklore">Religion, traditions and folklore</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cattle_in_religion" title="Cattle in religion">Cattle in religion</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hindu_tradition">Hindu tradition</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cattle_slaughter_in_India" title="Cattle slaughter in India">Cattle slaughter in India</a></div>
<p>Cattle are venerated within the <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindu</a> religion of India. In the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Vedic_religion" title="Historical Vedic religion">Vedic period</a> they were a symbol of plenty <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jha2002_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jha2002-209">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>130</span></sup> and were frequently slaughtered. In later times they gradually acquired their present status. According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a></i>, they are to be treated with the same respect 'as one's mother'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> In the middle of the first millennium, the consumption of beef began to be disfavoured by lawgivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jha2002_209-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jha2002-209">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>144</span></sup> Although there has never been any cow-goddesses or temples dedicated to them,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jha2002_209-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jha2002-209">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>146</span></sup> cows appear in numerous stories from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veda" title="Veda">Vedas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Purana" title="Purana">Puranas</a>. The deity <a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a> was brought up in a family of cowherders, and given the name <a href="/wiki/Govinda" title="Govinda">Govinda</a> (protector of the cows). Also, <a href="/wiki/Shiva" title="Shiva">Shiva</a> is traditionally said to ride on the back of a bull named <a href="/wiki/Nandi_(bull)" title="Nandi (bull)">Nandi</a>.
</p><p>Hinduism considers cow as divine and satvik (with pure virtues) and is worshipped as goddess. Medical science stresses importance of the cow for her milk, her urine as well as her excreta in our day-to-day life. Govidyapeetham, India aims to revive reproduction methodologies undertaken by Balram (brother of Lord Krishna, a deity in Hinduism) combining with modern technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aniruddhafoundation.com_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-aniruddhafoundation.com-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Govidyapeetham2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="455" data-file-width="749" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Govidyapeetham2.jpg/220px-Govidyapeetham2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Govidyapeetham2.jpg/330px-Govidyapeetham2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Govidyapeetham2.jpg/440px-Govidyapeetham2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Govidyapeetham2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ancient Cattle Reproduction Methodologies Revived at Govidyapeetham, India</div></div></div>
<p>Milk and milk products were used in Vedic rituals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jha2002_209-3"><a href="#cite_note-Jha2002-209">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>130</span></sup> In the postvedic period products of the cow – milk, curd, ghee, but also cow dung and urine (<a href="/wiki/Gomutra" title="Gomutra">gomutra</a>), or the combination of these five (<a href="/wiki/Panchagavya" title="Panchagavya">panchagavya</a>) – began to assume an increasingly important role in <a href="/wiki/Ritual_purification" title="Ritual purification">ritual purification</a> and expiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jha2002_209-4"><a href="#cite_note-Jha2002-209">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>130–1</span></sup>
</p><p>Veneration of the cow has become a symbol of the identity of Hindus as a community,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jha2002_209-5"><a href="#cite_note-Jha2002-209">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>20</span></sup> especially since the end of the 19th century. Slaughter of cows (including oxen, bulls and calves) is forbidden by law in several states of the Indian Union. <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s" title="McDonald's">McDonald's</a> outlets in India do not serve any beef burgers. In Maharaja <a href="/wiki/Ranjit_Singh" title="Ranjit Singh">Ranjit Singh</a>'s empire of the early 19th century, the killing of a cow was punishable by death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_traditions">Other traditions</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:UK_Durham_Dun-Cow.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="390" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/UK_Durham_Dun-Cow.jpg/170px-UK_Durham_Dun-Cow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/UK_Durham_Dun-Cow.jpg/255px-UK_Durham_Dun-Cow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/UK_Durham_Dun-Cow.jpg/340px-UK_Durham_Dun-Cow.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:UK_Durham_Dun-Cow.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Legend of the founding of <a href="/wiki/Durham_Cathedral" title="Durham Cathedral">Durham Cathedral</a> is that monks carrying the body of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Cuthbert" title="Saint Cuthbert">Saint Cuthbert</a> were led to the location by a milk maid who had lost her <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dun_cow" title="Dun cow">dun cow</a>, which was found resting on the spot.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Norske_Folkelivsbilleder_08_-_En_Aften_ved_S%C3%A6teren_(Knud_Bergslien).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2512" data-file-width="2932" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Norske_Folkelivsbilleder_08_-_En_Aften_ved_S%C3%A6teren_%28Knud_Bergslien%29.jpg/200px-Norske_Folkelivsbilleder_08_-_En_Aften_ved_S%C3%A6teren_%28Knud_Bergslien%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Norske_Folkelivsbilleder_08_-_En_Aften_ved_S%C3%A6teren_%28Knud_Bergslien%29.jpg/300px-Norske_Folkelivsbilleder_08_-_En_Aften_ved_S%C3%A6teren_%28Knud_Bergslien%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Norske_Folkelivsbilleder_08_-_En_Aften_ved_S%C3%A6teren_%28Knud_Bergslien%29.jpg/400px-Norske_Folkelivsbilleder_08_-_En_Aften_ved_S%C3%A6teren_%28Knud_Bergslien%29.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Norske_Folkelivsbilleder_08_-_En_Aften_ved_S%C3%A6teren_(Knud_Bergslien).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An idealized depiction of girl cow herders in 19th-century Norway by <a href="/wiki/Knud_Bergslien" title="Knud Bergslien">Knud Bergslien</a>.</div></div></div>
<ul><li>The Evangelist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Luke" title="St. Luke">St. Luke</a> is depicted as an ox in <a href="/wiki/Christian_art" title="Christian art">Christian art</a>.</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, as described in <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0419.htm#2" rel="nofollow">Numbers 19:2</a>, the ashes of a sacrificed unblemished <a href="/wiki/Red_heifer" title="Red heifer">red heifer</a> that has never been yoked can be used for <a href="/wiki/Ritual_purification" title="Ritual purification">ritual purification</a> of people who came into contact with a corpse.</li>
<li>The ox is one of the 12-year cycle of animals which appear in the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_zodiac" title="Chinese zodiac">Chinese zodiac</a> related to the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_calendar" title="Chinese calendar">Chinese calendar</a>. See: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ox_(Zodiac)" title="Ox (Zodiac)">Ox (Zodiac)</a>.</li>
<li>The constellation <a href="/wiki/Taurus_(constellation)" title="Taurus (constellation)">Taurus</a> represents a bull.</li>
<li>An apocryphal story has it that a cow started the <a href="/wiki/Great_Chicago_Fire" title="Great Chicago Fire">Great Chicago Fire</a> by kicking over a <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a> lamp. Michael Ahern, the reporter who created the cow story, admitted in 1893 that he had fabricated it for more colorful copy.</li>
<li>On 18 February 1930, <a href="/wiki/Elm_Farm_Ollie" title="Elm Farm Ollie">Elm Farm Ollie</a> became the first cow to fly in an airplane and also the first cow to be milked in an airplane.</li>
<li>The first known law requiring branding in North America was enacted on 5 February 1644, by Connecticut. It said that all cattle and pigs had to have a registered brand or earmark by 1 May 1644.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Akabeko" title="Akabeko">akabeko</a><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span lang="ja">赤べこ</span>, <i>red cow</i>)</span> is a traditional toy from the <a href="/wiki/Aizu" title="Aizu">Aizu</a> region of Japan that is thought to ward off illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup></li>
<li>The case of <i><a href="/wiki/Sherwood_v._Walker" title="Sherwood v. Walker">Sherwood v. Walker</a></i>—involving a supposedly barren heifer that was actually pregnant—-first enunciated the concept of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutual_mistake" title="Mutual mistake">mutual mistake</a> as a means of destroying the <a href="/wiki/Meeting_of_the_minds" title="Meeting of the minds">meeting of the minds</a> in contract law.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Fula_people" title="Fula people">Fulani</a> of West Africa are the world's largest nomadic cattle-herders.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Maasai_people" title="Maasai people">Maasai tribe</a> of East Africa traditionally believe their god <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Engai" title="Engai">Engai</a> entitled them to divine rights to the ownership of all cattle on earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_heraldry">In heraldry</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cio%C5%82ek_coat_of_arms" title="Ciołek coat of arms">Ciołek coat of arms</a></div>
<p>Cattle are typically represented in <a href="/wiki/Heraldry" title="Heraldry">heraldry</a> by the <b>bull</b>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Population">Population</span></h2>
<p>For 2013, the FAO estimated global cattle numbers at 1.47 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOSTAT_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAOSTAT-215">[215]</a></sup> Regionally, the FAO estimate for 2013 includes: Asia 495 million; South America 348 million; Africa 305 million; Europe 122 million; North America 102 million; Central America 46 million; Oceania 42 million; and Caribbean 9 million.  The following table shows the cattle population in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of  2003<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Cattle&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Africa had about 231 million head of cattle, raised in both traditional and non-traditional systems, but often an "integral" part of the culture and way of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup>
</p>
